<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_silver" title="Template:Infobox silver"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_silver" title="Template talk:Infobox silver"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_silver&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Silver</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Ag</b> (from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">argentum</i></i>, derived from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/h%E2%82%82er%C7%B5-" class="extiw" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/h₂erǵ-"><i>h₂erǵ</i></a>: "shiny" or "white") and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 47. A soft, white, lustrous <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a>, it exhibits the highest <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reflectivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Reflectivity">reflectivity</a> of any <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>. The metal is found in the Earth's crust in the pure, free elemental form ("native silver"), as an <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> with <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> and other metals, and in minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Argentite" title="Argentite">argentite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorargyrite" title="Chlorargyrite">chlorargyrite</a>. Most silver is produced as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, gold, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> <a href="/wiki/Refining" title="Refining">refining</a>.
</p><p>Silver has long been valued as a <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metal</a>. Silver metal is used in many <a href="/wiki/Bullion_coin" title="Bullion coin">bullion coins</a>, sometimes <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">alongside gold</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> while it is more abundant than gold, it is much less abundant as a <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">native metal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Its purity is typically measured on a <a href="/wiki/Per-mille" class="mw-redirect" title="Per-mille">per-mille</a> basis; a 94%-pure alloy is described as "0.940 fine". As one of the seven <a href="/wiki/Metals_of_antiquity" title="Metals of antiquity">metals of antiquity</a>, silver has had an enduring role in most human cultures.
</p><p>Other than in <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a> and as an <a href="/wiki/Investment" title="Investment">investment</a> medium (<a href="/wiki/Coins" class="mw-redirect" title="Coins">coins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> silver is used in <a href="/wiki/Solar_panels" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar panels">solar panels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_filtration" class="mw-redirect" title="Water filtration">water filtration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewellery" title="Jewellery">jewellery</a>, ornaments, high-value tableware and utensils (hence the term <a href="/wiki/Silver_(household)" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver (household)">silverware</a>), in <a href="/wiki/Electrical_contact" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical contact">electrical contacts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conductors</a>, in specialized mirrors, window coatings, in <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a> of chemical reactions, as a colorant in <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> and in specialised confectionery. Its compounds are used in <a href="/wiki/Photographic_film" title="Photographic film">photographic</a> and <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> film. Dilute solutions of <a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">silver nitrate</a> and other silver compounds are used as <a href="/wiki/Disinfectant" title="Disinfectant">disinfectants</a> and microbiocides (<a href="/wiki/Oligodynamic_effect" title="Oligodynamic effect">oligodynamic effect</a>), added to <a href="/wiki/Bandage" title="Bandage">bandages</a> and wound-dressings, <a href="/wiki/Catheter" title="Catheter">catheters</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Medical_instrument" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical instrument">medical instruments</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ag_atomic_wire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Ag_atomic_wire.jpg/170px-Ag_atomic_wire.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Ag_atomic_wire.jpg/255px-Ag_atomic_wire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Ag_atomic_wire.jpg/340px-Ag_atomic_wire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="886" data-file-height="1335" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ag_atomic_wire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver is extremely ductile, and can be drawn into a wire one atom wide.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Silver is similar in its physical and chemical properties to its two vertical neighbours in <a href="/wiki/Group_11_element" title="Group 11 element">group 11</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>. Its 47 electrons are arranged in the <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">configuration</a> [Kr]4d<sup>10</sup>5s<sup>1</sup>, similarly to copper ([Ar]3d<sup>10</sup>4s<sup>1</sup>) and gold ([Xe]4f<sup>14</sup>5d<sup>10</sup>6s<sup>1</sup>); group 11 is one of the few groups in the <a href="/wiki/D-block" class="mw-redirect" title="D-block">d-block</a> which has a completely consistent set of electron configurations.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> This distinctive electron configuration, with a single electron in the highest occupied s subshell over a filled d subshell, accounts for many of the singular properties of metallic silver.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1177_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1177-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver is an extremely soft, <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malleability" class="mw-redirect" title="Malleability">malleable</a> <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a>, though it is slightly less malleable than gold. Silver crystallizes in a <a href="/wiki/Face-centered_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Face-centered cubic">face-centered cubic</a> lattice with bulk coordination number 12, where only the single 5s electron is delocalized, similarly to copper and gold.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1178_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1178-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Unlike metals with incomplete d-shells, metallic bonds in silver are lacking a <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a> character and are relatively weak. This observation explains the low <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a> and high ductility of <a href="/wiki/Monocrystalline" class="mw-redirect" title="Monocrystalline">single crystals</a> of silver.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver has a brilliant white metallic luster that can take a high <a href="/wiki/Polishing" title="Polishing">polish</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-reflective_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reflective-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and which is so characteristic that the name of the metal itself has become a <a href="/wiki/Silver_(colour)" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver (colour)">colour name</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1177_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1177-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Unlike copper and gold, the energy required to excite an electron from the filled d band to the s-p conduction band in silver is large enough (around 385&#160;kJ/mol) that it no longer corresponds to absorption in the visible region of the spectrum, but rather in the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>; hence silver is not a coloured metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1177_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1177-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Protected silver has greater optical <a href="/wiki/Reflectivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Reflectivity">reflectivity</a> than <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> at all wavelengths longer than ~450&#160;nm.<sup id="cite_ref-edwards_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edwards-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> At wavelengths shorter than 450&#160;nm, silver's reflectivity is inferior to that of aluminium and drops to zero near 310&#160;nm.<sup id="cite_ref-gemin_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gemin-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Very high electrical and thermal conductivity is common to the elements in group 11, because their single s electron is free and does not interact with the filled d subshell, as such interactions (which occur in the preceding transition metals) lower electron mobility.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> of silver is the greatest of all metals, greater even than copper, but it is not widely used for this property because of the higher cost. An exception is in <a href="/wiki/Radio-frequency_engineering" title="Radio-frequency engineering">radio-frequency engineering</a>, particularly at <a href="/wiki/VHF" class="mw-redirect" title="VHF">VHF</a> and higher frequencies where silver plating improves electrical conductivity because those <a href="/wiki/Skin_effect" title="Skin effect">currents tend to flow on the surface of conductors</a> rather than through the interior. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in the US, <span style="white-space:nowrap">13540</span> tons of silver were used in <a href="/wiki/Electromagnets" class="mw-redirect" title="Electromagnets">electromagnets</a> for enriching <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, mainly because of the wartime shortage of copper.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Pure silver has the highest <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of any metal, although the conductivity of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> (in the <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotrope</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Superfluid_helium-4" title="Superfluid helium-4">superfluid helium-4</a> are even higher.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Silver also has the lowest <a href="/wiki/Contact_resistance" title="Contact resistance">contact resistance</a> of any metal.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver readily forms <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> with copper and gold, as well as <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>. Zinc-silver alloys with low zinc concentration may be considered as face-centred cubic solid solutions of zinc in silver, as the structure of the silver is largely unchanged while the electron concentration rises as more zinc is added. Increasing the electron concentration further leads to <a href="/wiki/Body-centred_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Body-centred cubic">body-centred cubic</a> (electron concentration 1.5), <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">complex cubic</a> (1.615), and <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_close-packed" class="mw-redirect" title="Hexagonal close-packed">hexagonal close-packed</a> phases (1.75).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1178_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1178-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_silver" title="Isotopes of silver">Isotopes of silver</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring silver is composed of two stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, <sup>107</sup>Ag and <sup>109</sup>Ag, with <sup>107</sup>Ag being slightly more abundant (51.839% <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a>). This almost equal abundance is rare in the periodic table. The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> is 107.8682(2) <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic mass unit">u</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-IUPAC_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> this value is very important because of the importance of silver compounds, particularly halides, in <a href="/wiki/Gravimetric_analysis" title="Gravimetric analysis">gravimetric analysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUPAC_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Both isotopes of silver are produced in stars via the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> (slow neutron capture), as well as in supernovas via the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a> (rapid neutron capture).<sup id="cite_ref-Cameron_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Twenty-eight <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized, the most stable being <sup>105</sup>Ag with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 41.29 days, <sup>111</sup>Ag with a half-life of 7.45 days, and <sup>112</sup>Ag with a half-life of 3.13 hours. Silver has numerous <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a>, the most stable being <sup>108m</sup>Ag (<i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub> = 418 years), <sup>110m</sup>Ag (<i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub> = 249.79 days) and <sup>106m</sup>Ag (<i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub> = 8.28 days). All of the remaining <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives of less than an hour, and the majority of these have half-lives of less than three minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-Audi_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Isotopes of silver range in <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">relative atomic mass</a> from 92.950&#160;u (<sup>93</sup>Ag) to 129.950&#160;u (<sup>130</sup>Ag);<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> the primary <a href="/wiki/Decay_mode" class="mw-redirect" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> before the most abundant stable isotope, <sup>107</sup>Ag, is <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> and the primary mode after is <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>. The primary <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay products</a> before <sup>107</sup>Ag are <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a> (element 46) isotopes, and the primary products after are <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> (element 48) isotopes.<sup id="cite_ref-Audi_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The palladium <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> <sup>107</sup>Pd decays by beta emission to <sup>107</sup>Ag with a half-life of 6.5 million years. <a href="/wiki/Iron_meteorite" title="Iron meteorite">Iron meteorites</a> are the only objects with a high-enough palladium-to-silver ratio to yield measurable variations in <sup>107</sup>Ag abundance. <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiogenic">Radiogenic</a> <sup>107</sup>Ag was first discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara,_Durango" title="Santa Clara, Durango">Santa Clara</a> meteorite in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The discoverers suggest the coalescence and differentiation of iron-cored small <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> may have occurred 10 million years after a <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Nucleosynthetic">nucleosynthetic</a> event. <sup>107</sup>Pd–<sup>107</sup>Ag correlations observed in bodies that have clearly been melted since the <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">solar system</a> must reflect the presence of unstable nuclides in the early solar system.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; clear:right; margin-left:1em; margin-top:0;">

<p>Silver is a rather unreactive metal. This is because its filled 4d shell is not very effective in shielding the electrostatic forces of attraction from the nucleus to the outermost 5s electron, and hence silver is near the bottom of the <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrochemical series">electrochemical series</a> (<i>E</i><sup>0</sup>(Ag<sup>+</sup>/Ag) = +0.799&#160;V).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1177_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1177-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In group 11, silver has the lowest first ionization energy (showing the instability of the 5s orbital), but has higher second and third ionization energies than copper and gold (showing the stability of the 4d orbitals), so that the chemistry of silver is predominantly that of the +1 oxidation state, reflecting the increasingly limited range of oxidation states along the transition series as the d-orbitals fill and stabilize.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1180_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1180-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Unlike copper, for which the larger <a href="/wiki/Hydration_energy" title="Hydration energy">hydration energy</a> of Cu<sup>2+</sup> as compared to Cu<sup>+</sup> is the reason why the former is the more stable in aqueous solution and solids despite lacking the stable filled d-subshell of the latter, with silver this effect is swamped by its larger second ionisation energy. Hence, Ag<sup>+</sup> is the stable species in aqueous solution and solids, with Ag<sup>2+</sup> being much less stable as it oxidizes water.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1180_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1180-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most silver compounds have significant <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a> character due to the small size and high first ionization energy (730.8&#160;kJ/mol) of silver.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1177_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1177-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, silver's Pauling <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> of 1.93 is higher than that of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> (1.87), and its <a href="/wiki/Electron_affinity" title="Electron affinity">electron affinity</a> of 125.6&#160;kJ/mol is much higher than that of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (72.8&#160;kJ/mol) and not much less than that of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (141.0&#160;kJ/mol).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1176_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1176-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Due to its full d-subshell, silver in its main +1 oxidation state exhibits relatively few properties of the transition metals proper from groups 4 to 10, forming rather unstable <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Organometallic compound">organometallic compounds</a>, forming linear complexes showing very low <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination numbers</a> like 2, and forming an amphoteric oxide<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Zintl_phase" title="Zintl phase">Zintl phases</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Unlike the preceding transition metals, the +1 oxidation state of silver is stable even in the absence of <a href="/wiki/Pi_backbonding" title="Pi backbonding">π-acceptor ligands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1180_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1180-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver does not react with air, even at red heat, and thus was considered by <a href="/wiki/Alchemist" class="mw-redirect" title="Alchemist">alchemists</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Noble_metal" title="Noble metal">noble metal</a> along with gold. Its reactivity is intermediate between that of copper (which forms <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_oxide" title="Copper(I) oxide">copper(I) oxide</a> when heated in air to red heat) and gold. Like copper, silver reacts with <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and its compounds; in their presence, silver tarnishes in air to form the black <a href="/wiki/Silver_sulfide" title="Silver sulfide">silver sulfide</a> (copper forms the green <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a> instead, while gold does not react). Unlike copper, silver will not react with the halogens, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> gas, with which it forms the <a href="/wiki/Silver(II)_fluoride" title="Silver(II) fluoride">difluoride</a>. While silver is not attacked by non-oxidizing acids, the metal dissolves readily in hot concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, as well as dilute or concentrated <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>. In the presence of air, and especially in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, silver dissolves readily in aqueous solutions of <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1179_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1179-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The three main forms of deterioration in historical silver artifacts are tarnishing, formation of <a href="/wiki/Silver_chloride" title="Silver chloride">silver chloride</a> due to long-term immersion in salt water, as well as reaction with <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> ions or oxygen. Fresh silver chloride is pale yellow, becoming purplish on exposure to light; it projects slightly from the surface of the artifact or coin. The precipitation of copper in ancient silver can be used to date artifacts, as copper is nearly always a constituent of silver alloys.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver metal is attacked by strong oxidizers such as <a href="/wiki/Potassium_permanganate" title="Potassium permanganate">potassium permanganate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">KMnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_dichromate" title="Potassium dichromate">potassium dichromate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Cr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span></span>), and in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_bromide" title="Potassium bromide">potassium bromide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">KBr</span>). These compounds are used in photography to <a href="/wiki/Bleach" title="Bleach">bleach</a> silver images, converting them to silver bromide that can either be fixed with <a href="/wiki/Thiosulfate" title="Thiosulfate">thiosulfate</a> or redeveloped to <a href="/wiki/Potassium_dichromate#photography" title="Potassium dichromate">intensify</a> the original image. Silver forms <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a> complexes (<a href="/wiki/Silver_cyanide" title="Silver cyanide">silver cyanide</a>) that are soluble in water in the presence of an excess of cyanide ions. Silver cyanide solutions are used in <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> of silver.<sup id="cite_ref-photo_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-photo-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The common <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> of silver are (in order of commonness): +1 (the most stable state; for example, <a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">silver nitrate</a>, AgNO<sub>3</sub>); +2 (highly oxidising; for example, <a href="/wiki/Silver(II)_fluoride" title="Silver(II) fluoride">silver(II) fluoride</a>, AgF<sub>2</sub>); and even very rarely +3 (extreme oxidising; for example, potassium tetrafluoroargentate(III), KAgF<sub>4</sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The +1 state is by far the most common, followed by the easily reducible +2 state. The +3 state requires very strong oxidising agents to attain, such as <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Peroxodisulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Peroxodisulfate">peroxodisulfate</a>, and some silver(III) compounds react with atmospheric moisture and attack glass.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1188_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1188-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, silver(III) fluoride is usually obtained by reacting silver or silver monofluoride with the strongest known oxidizing agent, <a href="/wiki/Krypton_difluoride" title="Krypton difluoride">krypton difluoride</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood903_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood903-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_and_chalcogenides">Oxides and chalcogenides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Oxides and chalcogenides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sulfid_st%C5%99%C3%ADbrn%C3%BD.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sulfid_st%C5%99%C3%ADbrn%C3%BD.PNG/220px-Sulfid_st%C5%99%C3%ADbrn%C3%BD.PNG" width="220" height="82" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sulfid_st%C5%99%C3%ADbrn%C3%BD.PNG/330px-Sulfid_st%C5%99%C3%ADbrn%C3%BD.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sulfid_st%C5%99%C3%ADbrn%C3%BD.PNG/440px-Sulfid_st%C5%99%C3%ADbrn%C3%BD.PNG 2x" data-file-width="628" data-file-height="233" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sulfid_st%C5%99%C3%ADbrn%C3%BD.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver(I) sulfide</div></div></div>
<p>Silver and gold have rather low <a href="/wiki/Chemical_affinity" title="Chemical affinity">chemical affinities</a> for oxygen, lower than copper, and it is therefore expected that silver oxides are thermally quite unstable. Soluble silver(I) salts precipitate dark-brown <a href="/wiki/Silver(I)_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver(I) oxide">silver(I) oxide</a>, Ag<sub>2</sub>O, upon the addition of alkali. (The hydroxide AgOH exists only in solution; otherwise it spontaneously decomposes to the oxide.) Silver(I) oxide is very easily reduced to metallic silver, and decomposes to silver and oxygen above 160&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1181_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1181-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> This and other silver(I) compounds may be oxidized by the strong oxidizing agent <a href="/wiki/Peroxodisulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Peroxodisulfate">peroxodisulfate</a> to black AgO, a mixed <a href="/wiki/Silver(I,III)_oxide" title="Silver(I,III) oxide">silver(I,III) oxide</a> of formula Ag<sup>I</sup>Ag<sup>III</sup>O<sub>2</sub>. Some other mixed oxides with silver in non-integral oxidation states, namely Ag<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Ag<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, are also known, as is Ag<sub>3</sub>O which behaves as a metallic conductor.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1181_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1181-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Silver(I)_sulfide" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver(I) sulfide">Silver(I) sulfide</a>, Ag<sub>2</sub>S, is very readily formed from its constituent elements and is the cause of the black tarnish on some old silver objects. It may also be formed from the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> with silver metal or aqueous Ag<sup>+</sup> ions. Many non-stoichiometric <a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">tellurides</a> are known; in particular, AgTe<sub>~3</sub> is a low-temperature <a href="/wiki/Superconductor" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconductor">superconductor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1181_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1181-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silver_halide" title="Silver halide">Silver halide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Silver_Halide_Precipitates.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/Common_Silver_Halide_Precipitates.jpg/220px-Common_Silver_Halide_Precipitates.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/Common_Silver_Halide_Precipitates.jpg/330px-Common_Silver_Halide_Precipitates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/Common_Silver_Halide_Precipitates.jpg/440px-Common_Silver_Halide_Precipitates.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Silver_Halide_Precipitates.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The three common silver halide precipitates: from left to right, <a href="/wiki/Silver_iodide" title="Silver iodide">silver iodide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver_bromide" title="Silver bromide">silver bromide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silver_chloride" title="Silver chloride">silver chloride</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The only known dihalide of silver is <a href="/wiki/Silver(II)_fluoride" title="Silver(II) fluoride">the difluoride</a>, AgF<sub>2</sub>, which can be obtained from the elements under heat. A strong yet thermally stable and therefore safe fluorinating agent, silver(II) fluoride is often used to synthesize <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbon" title="Hydrofluorocarbon">hydrofluorocarbons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1183_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1183-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In stark contrast to this, all four silver(I) halides are known. The <a href="/wiki/Silver(I)_fluoride" title="Silver(I) fluoride">fluoride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver_chloride" title="Silver chloride">chloride</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silver_bromide" title="Silver bromide">bromide</a> have the sodium chloride structure, but the <a href="/wiki/Silver_iodide" title="Silver iodide">iodide</a> has three known stable forms at different temperatures; that at room temperature is the cubic <a href="/wiki/Zinc_blende" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc blende">zinc blende</a> structure. They can all be obtained by the direct reaction of their respective elements.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1183_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1183-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> As the halogen group is descended, the silver halide gains more and more covalent character, solubility decreases, and the color changes from the white chloride to the yellow iodide as the energy required for <a href="/wiki/Charge-transfer_complex" title="Charge-transfer complex">ligand-metal charge transfer</a> (X<sup>−</sup>Ag<sup>+</sup> → XAg) decreases.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1183_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1183-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The fluoride is anomalous, as the fluoride ion is so small that it has a considerable <a href="/wiki/Solvation" title="Solvation">solvation</a> energy and hence is highly water-soluble and forms di- and tetrahydrates.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1183_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1183-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The other three silver halides are highly insoluble in aqueous solutions and are very commonly used in gravimetric <a href="/wiki/Wet_chemistry" title="Wet chemistry">analytical</a> methods.<sup id="cite_ref-IUPAC_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> All four are <a href="/wiki/Photosensitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Photosensitive">photosensitive</a> (though the monofluoride is so only to <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light), especially the bromide and iodide which photodecompose to silver metal, and thus were used in traditional photography.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1183_37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1183-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The reaction involved is:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1185_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1185-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>X<sup>−</sup> + <i>hν</i> → X + e<sup>−</sup> (excitation of the halide ion, which gives up its extra electron into the conduction band)</dd>
<dd>Ag<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup> → Ag (liberation of a silver ion, which gains an electron to become a silver atom)</dd></dl>
<p>The process is not reversible because the silver atom liberated is typically found at a <a href="/wiki/Crystal_defect" class="mw-redirect" title="Crystal defect">crystal defect</a> or an impurity site, so that the electron's energy is lowered enough that it is "trapped".<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1185_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1185-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_inorganic_compounds">Other inorganic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Other inorganic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_nitrate_crystals.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Silver_nitrate_crystals.jpg/100px-Silver_nitrate_crystals.jpg" width="100" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Silver_nitrate_crystals.jpg/150px-Silver_nitrate_crystals.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Silver_nitrate_crystals.jpg/200px-Silver_nitrate_crystals.jpg 2x" data-file-width="336" data-file-height="494" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_nitrate_crystals.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crystals of silver nitrate</div></div></div>
<p>White <a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">silver nitrate</a>, AgNO<sub>3</sub>, is a versatile precursor to many other silver compounds, especially the halides, and is much less sensitive to light. It was once called <i>lunar caustic</i> because silver was called <i>luna</i> by the ancient alchemists, who believed that silver was associated with the moon.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> It is often used for gravimetric analysis, exploiting the insolubility of the heavier silver halides which it is a common precursor to.<sup id="cite_ref-IUPAC_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Silver nitrate is used in many ways in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>, e.g. for <a href="/wiki/Deprotection" class="mw-redirect" title="Deprotection">deprotection</a> and oxidations. Ag<sup>+</sup> binds <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a> reversibly, and silver nitrate has been used to separate mixtures of alkenes by selective absorption. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adduct</a> can be decomposed with <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> to release the free alkene.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Yellow <a href="/wiki/Silver_carbonate" title="Silver carbonate">silver carbonate</a>, Ag<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> can be easily prepared by reacting aqueous solutions of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a> with a deficiency of silver nitrate.<sup id="cite_ref-OS_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OS-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Its principal use is for the production of silver powder for use in microelectronics. It is reduced with <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, producing silver free of alkali metals:<sup id="cite_ref-Ull_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ull-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Ag<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> + CH<sub>2</sub>O → 2 Ag + 2 CO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Silver carbonate is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Reagent" title="Reagent">reagent</a> in organic synthesis such as the <a href="/wiki/Koenigs-Knorr_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Koenigs-Knorr reaction">Koenigs-Knorr reaction</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9tizon_oxidation" title="Fétizon oxidation">Fétizon oxidation</a>, silver carbonate on <a href="/wiki/Celite" class="mw-redirect" title="Celite">celite</a> acts as an <a href="/wiki/Oxidising_agent" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidising agent">oxidising agent</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Lactone" title="Lactone">lactones</a> from <a href="/wiki/Diols" class="mw-redirect" title="Diols">diols</a>. It is also employed to convert <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> bromides into <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OS_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OS-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Silver_fulminate" title="Silver fulminate">Silver fulminate</a>, AgCNO, a powerful, touch-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosive</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Percussion_cap" title="Percussion cap">percussion caps</a>, is made by reaction of silver metal with nitric acid in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>. Other dangerously explosive silver compounds are <a href="/wiki/Silver_azide" title="Silver azide">silver azide</a>, AgN<sub>3</sub>, formed by reaction of <a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">silver nitrate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_azide" title="Sodium azide">sodium azide</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Silver_acetylide" title="Silver acetylide">silver acetylide</a>, Ag<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>, formed when silver reacts with <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> gas in <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> solution.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1180_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1180-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In its most characteristic reaction, silver azide decomposes explosively, releasing nitrogen gas: given the photosensitivity of silver salts, this behaviour may be induced by shining a light on its crystals.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1180_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1180-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">AgN<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> (s) → 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> (g) + 2 Ag (s)</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_compounds">Coordination compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Coordination compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diamminesilver(I)-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png/220px-Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png/330px-Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png/440px-Diamminesilver%28I%29-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="602" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diamminesilver(I)-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the diamminesilver(I) complex, [Ag(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup></div></div></div>
<p>Silver complexes tend to be similar to those of its lighter homologue copper. Silver(III) complexes tend to be rare and very easily reduced to the more stable lower oxidation states, though they are slightly more stable than those of copper(III). For instance, the square planar periodate [Ag(IO<sub>5</sub>OH)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>5−</sup> and tellurate [Ag{TeO<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub>}<sub>2</sub>]<sup>5−</sup> complexes may be prepared by oxidising silver(I) with alkaline <a href="/wiki/Peroxodisulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Peroxodisulfate">peroxodisulfate</a>. The yellow diamagnetic [AgF<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup> is much less stable, fuming in moist air and reacting with glass.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1188_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1188-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver(II) complexes are more common. Like the valence isoelectronic copper(II) complexes, they are usually square planar and paramagnetic, which is increased by the greater field splitting for 4d electrons than for 3d electrons. Aqueous Ag<sup>2+</sup>, produced by oxidation of Ag<sup>+</sup> by ozone, is a very strong oxidising agent, even in acidic solutions: it is stabilized in <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acid</a> due to complex formation. Peroxodisulfate oxidation is generally necessary to give the more stable complexes with heterocyclic <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>, such as [Ag(py)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> and [Ag(bipy)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>2+</sup>: these are stable provided the counterion cannot reduce the silver back to the +1 oxidation state. [AgF<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2−</sup> is also known in its violet barium salt, as are some silver(II) complexes with <i>N</i>- or <i>O</i>-donor ligands such as pyridine carboxylates.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1189_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1189-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By far the most important oxidation state for silver in complexes is +1. The Ag<sup>+</sup> cation is diamagnetic, like its homologues Cu<sup>+</sup> and Au<sup>+</sup>, as all three have closed-shell electron configurations with no unpaired electrons: its complexes are colourless provided the ligands are not too easily polarized such as I<sup>−</sup>. Ag<sup>+</sup> forms salts with most anions, but it is reluctant to coordinate to oxygen and thus most of these salts are insoluble in water: the exceptions are the nitrate, perchlorate, and fluoride. The tetracoordinate tetrahedral aqueous ion [Ag(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>+</sup> is known, but the characteristic geometry for the Ag<sup>+</sup> cation is 2-coordinate linear. For example, silver chloride dissolves readily in excess aqueous ammonia to form [Ag(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup>; silver salts are dissolved in photography due to the formation of the thiosulfate complex [Ag(S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>3−</sup>; and <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a> extraction for silver (and gold) works by the formation of the complex [Ag(CN)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>−</sup>. Silver cyanide forms the linear polymer {Ag–C≡N→Ag–C≡N→}; silver <a href="/wiki/Thiocyanate" title="Thiocyanate">thiocyanate</a> has a similar structure, but forms a zigzag instead because of the sp<sup>3</sup>-<a href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbital hybridization">hybridized</a> sulfur atom. <a href="/wiki/Chelating_ligand" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelating ligand">Chelating ligands</a> are unable to form linear complexes and thus silver(I) complexes with them tend to form polymers; a few exceptions exist, such as the near-tetrahedral <a href="/wiki/Diphosphine" class="mw-redirect" title="Diphosphine">diphosphine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diarsine" title="Diarsine">diarsine</a> complexes [Ag(L–L)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1195_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1195-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organometallic">Organometallic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Organometallic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organosilver_chemistry" title="Organosilver chemistry">Organosilver chemistry</a></div>
<p>Under standard conditions, silver does not form simple carbonyls, due to the weakness of the Ag–C bond. A few are known at very low temperatures around 6–15&#160;K, such as the green, planar paramagnetic Ag(CO)<sub>3</sub>, which dimerizes at 25–30&#160;K, probably by forming Ag–Ag bonds. Additionally, the silver carbonyl [Ag(CO)] [B(OTeF<sub>5</sub>)<sub>4</sub>] is known. Polymeric AgLX complexes with <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkyne" title="Alkyne">alkynes</a> are known, but their bonds are thermodynamically weaker than even those of the <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> complexes (though they are formed more readily than those of the analogous gold complexes): they are also quite unsymmetrical, showing the weak <i>π</i> bonding in group 11. Ag–C <i>σ</i> bonds may also be formed by silver(I), like copper(I) and gold(I), but the simple alkyls and aryls of silver(I) are even less stable than those of copper(I) (which tend to explode under ambient conditions). For example, poor thermal stability is reflected in the relative decomposition temperatures of AgMe (−50&#160;°C) and CuMe (−15&#160;°C) as well as those of PhAg (74&#160;°C) and PhCu (100&#160;°C).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1199_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1199-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The C–Ag bond is stabilized by <a href="/wiki/Perfluoroalkane" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfluoroalkane">perfluoroalkyl</a> ligands, for example in AgCF(CF<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller1968_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller1968-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Alkenylsilver compounds are also more stable than their alkylsilver counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Silver-<a href="/wiki/Transition_metal_carbene_complex" title="Transition metal carbene complex">NHC complexes</a> are easily prepared, and are commonly used to prepare other NHC complexes by displacing labile ligands. For example, the reaction of the bis(NHC)silver(I) complex with <a href="/wiki/Bis(acetonitrile)palladium_dichloride" title="Bis(acetonitrile)palladium dichloride">bis(acetonitrile)palladium dichloride</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chlorido(dimethyl_sulfide)gold(I)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chlorido(dimethyl sulfide)gold(I)">chlorido(dimethyl sulfide)gold(I)</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/File:Silver-NHC_as_carbene_transmetallation_agent.png" class="image"><img alt="Silver-NHC as carbene transmetallation agent.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Silver-NHC_as_carbene_transmetallation_agent.png/610px-Silver-NHC_as_carbene_transmetallation_agent.png" width="610" height="178" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Silver-NHC_as_carbene_transmetallation_agent.png/915px-Silver-NHC_as_carbene_transmetallation_agent.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Silver-NHC_as_carbene_transmetallation_agent.png/1220px-Silver-NHC_as_carbene_transmetallation_agent.png 2x" data-file-width="5846" data-file-height="1709" /></a></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intermetallic">Intermetallic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Intermetallic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg/250px-Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg.png" width="250" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg/375px-Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg/500px-Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="305" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different colors of silver–copper–gold alloys</div></div></div>
<p>Silver forms <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> with most other elements on the periodic table. The elements from groups 1–3, except for <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>, are very miscible with silver in the condensed phase and form intermetallic compounds; those from groups 4–9 are only poorly miscible; the elements in groups 10–14 (except <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>) have very complex Ag–M phase diagrams and form the most commercially important alloys; and the remaining elements on the periodic table have no consistency in their Ag–M phase diagrams. By far the most important such alloys are those with copper: most silver used for coinage and jewellery is in reality a silver–copper alloy, and the <a href="/wiki/Eutectic_mixture" class="mw-redirect" title="Eutectic mixture">eutectic mixture</a> is used in vacuum <a href="/wiki/Brazing" title="Brazing">brazing</a>. The two metals are completely miscible as liquids but not as solids; their importance in industry comes from the fact that their properties tend to be suitable over a wide range of variation in silver and copper concentration, although most useful alloys tend to be richer in silver than the eutectic mixture (71.9% silver and 28.1% copper by weight, and 60.1% silver and 28.1% copper by atom).<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann54_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann54-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most other binary alloys are of little use: for example, silver–gold alloys are too soft and silver–<a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> alloys too toxic. Ternary alloys have much greater importance: dental <a href="/wiki/Amalgam_(dentistry)" title="Amalgam (dentistry)">amalgams</a> are usually silver–tin–mercury alloys, silver–copper–gold alloys are very important in jewellery (usually on the gold-rich side) and have a vast range of hardnesses and colours, silver–copper–zinc alloys are useful as low-melting brazing alloys, and silver–cadmium–<a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a> (involving three adjacent elements on the periodic table) is useful in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> because of its high thermal neutron capture <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">cross-section</a>, good conduction of heat, mechanical stability, and resistance to corrosion in hot water.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann54_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann54-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word "silver" appears in <a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Anglo-Saxon</a> in various spellings, such as <i>seolfor</i> and <i>siolfor</i>. A similar form is seen throughout the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> (compare <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>silabar</i> and <i>silbir</i>). The chemical symbol Ag is from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word for "silver", <i>argentum</i> (compare <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> ἄργυρος, <i>árgyros</i>), from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> root *<i>h₂erǵ-</i> (formerly reconstructed as <i>*arǵ-</i>), meaning "white" or "shining": this was the usual Proto-Indo-European word for the metal, whose reflexes are missing in Germanic and <a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic" class="mw-redirect" title="Balto-Slavic">Balto-Slavic</a>. The Balto-Slavic words for silver are quite similar to the Germanic ones (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <i>серебро</i> [<i>serebro</i>], <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> <i>srebro</i>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a> <i>sidabras</i>) and they may have a common origin, although this is uncertain: some scholars have suggested the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> <i>sarpu</i> "refined silver" as this origin, related to the word <i>sarapu</i> "to refine or smelt".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vad%C3%A1szt%C3%A1l_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Vad%C3%A1szt%C3%A1l_%282%29.jpg/220px-Vad%C3%A1szt%C3%A1l_%282%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Vad%C3%A1szt%C3%A1l_%282%29.jpg/330px-Vad%C3%A1szt%C3%A1l_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Vad%C3%A1szt%C3%A1l_%282%29.jpg/440px-Vad%C3%A1szt%C3%A1l_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vad%C3%A1szt%C3%A1l_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver plate from the 4th century</div></div></div>
<p>Silver was one of the seven <a href="/wiki/Metals_of_antiquity" title="Metals of antiquity">metals of antiquity</a> that were known to prehistoric humans and whose discovery is thus lost to history.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks4_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks4-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In particular, the three metals of group 11, copper, silver, and gold, occur in the <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">elemental form</a> in nature and were probably used as the first primitive forms of <a href="/wiki/Money" title="Money">money</a> as opposed to simple bartering.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1173_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1173-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> However, unlike copper, silver did not lead to the growth of <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a> on account of its low structural strength, and was more often used ornamentally or as money.<sup id="cite_ref-metallurgy_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metallurgy-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Since silver is more reactive than gold, supplies of native silver were much more limited than those of gold.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1173_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1173-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> For example, silver was more expensive than gold in Egypt until around the fifteenth century BC:<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks14_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks14-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> the Egyptians are thought to have separated gold from silver by heating the metals with salt, and then reducing the <a href="/wiki/Silver_chloride" title="Silver chloride">silver chloride</a> produced to the metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann16_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann16-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The situation changed with the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Cupellation" title="Cupellation">cupellation</a>, a technique that allowed silver metal to be extracted from its ores. While <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a> heaps found in <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and on the islands of the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a> indicate that silver was being separated from <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> as early as the <a href="/wiki/4th_millennium_BC" title="4th millennium BC">4th millennium BC</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and one of the earliest silver extraction centres in Europe was <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> in early the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Chalcolithic period">Chalcolithic period</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> these techniques did not spread widely until later,
when it spread throughout the region and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks14_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks14-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The origins of silver production in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> were almost certainly equally ancient, but are not well-documented due to their great age.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann16_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann16-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_mining_in_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora_1490s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Silver_mining_in_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora_1490s.jpg/220px-Silver_mining_in_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora_1490s.jpg" width="220" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Silver_mining_in_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora_1490s.jpg/330px-Silver_mining_in_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora_1490s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Silver_mining_in_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora_1490s.jpg/440px-Silver_mining_in_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora_1490s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1412" data-file-height="2056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_mining_in_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora_1490s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver mining and processing in <a href="/wiki/Kutn%C3%A1_Hora" title="Kutná Hora">Kutná Hora</a>, Bohemia, 1490s</div></div></div>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> first came to what is now <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, they obtained so much silver that they could not fit it all on their ships, and as a result used silver to weight their anchors instead of lead.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks14_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks14-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> By the time of the Greek and Roman civilizations, silver coins were a staple of the economy:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood1173_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1173-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> the Greeks were already extracting silver from <a href="/wiki/Galena" title="Galena">galena</a> by the 7th century BC,<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks14_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks14-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and the rise of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> was partly made possible by the nearby silver mines at <a href="/wiki/Laurium" title="Laurium">Laurium</a>, from which they extracted about 30 tonnes a year from 600 to 300 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The stability of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman currency</a> relied to a high degree on the supply of silver bullion, mostly from Spain, which <a href="/wiki/Roman_metallurgy" title="Roman metallurgy">Roman miners</a> produced on a scale unparalleled before the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_New_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Discovery of the New World">discovery of the New World</a>. Reaching a peak production of 200 tonnes per year, an estimated silver stock of 10000 tonnes circulated in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_economy" title="Roman economy">Roman economy</a> in the middle of the second century AD, five to ten times larger than the combined amount of silver available to <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">medieval Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> around AD 800.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The Romans also recorded the extraction of silver in central and northern Europe in the same time period. This production came to a nearly complete halt with the fall of the Roman Empire, not to resume until the time of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>: by then, tens of thousands of tonnes of silver had already been extracted.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann16_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann16-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Central Europe became the centre of silver production during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, as the Mediterranean deposits exploited by the ancient civilisations had been exhausted. Silver mines were opened in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erzgebirge" class="mw-redirect" title="Erzgebirge">Erzgebirge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lahn" title="Lahn">Lahn</a> region, <a href="/wiki/Siegerland" title="Siegerland">Siegerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steiermark" class="mw-redirect" title="Steiermark">Steiermark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salzburg" title="Salzburg">Salzburg</a>, and the southern <a href="/wiki/Black_Forest" title="Black Forest">Black Forest</a>. Most of these ores were quite rich in silver and could simply be separated by hand from the remaining rock and then smelted; some deposits of native silver were also encountered. Many of these mines were soon exhausted, but a few of them remained active until the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, before which the world production of silver was around a meagre 50&#160;tonnes per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann16_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann16-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In the Americas, high temperature silver-lead <a href="/wiki/Cupellation" title="Cupellation">cupellation</a> technology was developed by pre-Inca civilizations as early as AD 60–120; silver deposits in India, China, Japan, and pre-Columbian America continued to be mined during this time.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann16_57-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann16-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the discovery of America and the plundering of silver by the Spanish conquistadors, Central and South America became the dominant producers of silver until around the beginning of the 18th century, particularly <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann16_57-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann16-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> the last of these countries later took its name from that of the metal that composed so much of its mineral wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The silver trade this was a part of gave way to a global network of exchange. As one historian put it, silver "went round the world and made the world go round."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Much of this silver ended up in the hands of the Chinese. A Portuguese merchant in 1621 noted that silver "wanders throughout all the world... before flocking to China, where it remains as if at its natural center."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Still, much of it went to Spain, allowing Spanish rulers to pursue military and political ambitions in both Europe and the Americas. "New World mines," concluded several historians, "supported the Spanish empire."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 19th century, primary production of silver moved to North America, particularly <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>: some secondary production from lead and zinc ores also took place in Europe, and deposits in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> were mined.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann16_57-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann16-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> emerged as an important producer during the 1970s after the discovery of copper deposits that were rich in silver, before the centre of production returned to the Americas the following decade. Today, Peru and Mexico are still among the primary silver producers, but the distribution of silver production around the world is quite balanced and about one-fifth of the silver supply comes from recycling instead of new production.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann16_57-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann16-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic_role">Symbolic role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Symbolic role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG/220px-6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG/330px-6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG/440px-6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1384" data-file-height="2076" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>16th-century fresco painting of Judas being paid thirty pieces of silver for his betrayal of Jesus</div></div></div>
<p>Silver plays a certain role in mythology and has found various usage as a metaphor and in folklore. The Greek poet <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Works_and_Days" title="Works and Days">Works and Days</a></i> (lines 109–201) lists different <a href="/wiki/Ages_of_man" class="mw-redirect" title="Ages of man">ages of man</a> named after metals like gold, silver, bronze and iron to account for successive ages of humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i> contains another retelling of the story, containing an illustration of silver's metaphorical use of signifying the second-best in a series, better than bronze but worse than gold:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>But when good <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn</a>, banish'd from above,<br />
Was driv'n to Hell, the world was under <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jove</a>.<br />
Succeeding times a silver age behold,<br />
Excelling brass, but more excell'd by gold.
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Ovid, <a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a>, Book I, trans. <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In folklore, silver was commonly thought to have mystic powers: for example, a <a href="/wiki/Bullet" title="Bullet">bullet</a> cast from silver is often supposed in such folklore the only weapon that is effective against a <a href="/wiki/Werewolf" title="Werewolf">werewolf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Witch" class="mw-redirect" title="Witch">witch</a>, or other <a href="/wiki/Monster" title="Monster">monsters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-narodna_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-narodna-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> From this the idiom of a <a href="/wiki/Silver_bullet" title="Silver bullet">silver bullet</a> developed into figuratively referring to any simple solution with very high effectiveness or almost miraculous results, as in the widely discussed <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> paper <i><a href="/wiki/No_Silver_Bullet" title="No Silver Bullet">No Silver Bullet</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver production has also inspired figurative language. Clear references to cupellation occur throughout the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah" title="Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a>'s rebuke to Judah: "The bellows are burned, the lead is consumed of the fire; the founder melteth in vain: for the wicked are not plucked away. Reprobate silver shall men call them, because the Lord hath rejected them." (Jeremiah 6:19–20) Jeremiah was also aware of sheet silver, exemplifying the malleability and ductility of the metal: "Silver spread into plates is brought from Tarshish, and gold from Uphaz, the work of the workman, and of the hands of the founder: blue and purple is their clothing: they are all the work of cunning men." (Jeremiah 10:9)<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks14_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks14-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver also has more negative cultural meanings: the idiom <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/thirty_pieces_of_silver" class="extiw" title="wikt:thirty pieces of silver">thirty pieces of silver</a>, referring to a reward for betrayal, references the bribe <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">Judas Iscariot</a> is said in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> to have taken from Jewish leaders in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> to turn <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus of Nazareth</a> over to soldiers of the high priest Caiaphas.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Ethically, silver also symbolizes greed and degradation of consciousness; this is the negative aspect, the perverting of its value.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Occurrence and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Silver_mining" title="Silver mining">Silver mining</a></div>
